Hello to everyone:

My name's Henry and I've been reading posts here for a while but have 
been waiting to contribute until I fully got the feel for what you all 
talk about.  I'm always stimulated by the discussions here. I think I'm 
now ready to start doing some talking too.

I'm a S.F. native but have been attending UC Santa Barabara for the past 
four years.  I go back to Frisco when I can and take in the extensive 
nightlife as deeply as possible.

Well, here in Santa Barbara, we have a very small, start-up scene run mostly 
by a group of two or three really cool people who know what's up.  They 
know how to throw a party and understand what it's all about.  The other 
night, they invited us to their small warehouse which has become the home for
their parties called the Tests.  This past Saturday was Test II.

To make a long story short, around midnight a dude with a knife threatened 
someone people outside and the Test people called the cops.  Raves are strictly 
forbidden in Santa Barabara so this was a last ditch, 
reacting-to-a-crisis sort of reaction.  To no one's suprise, the cops 
told us all to leave and the organizers are facing criminal charges for 
throwing the party.

Here is my main question.  I was thinking afterwards that we should have 
collectively sat-in to protect and assert our rights.  This was a peaceful 
gathering at which one overzealous, high-strung person ruined it for everyone.  
It frustrated me that I nor anyone else thought of the option to sit-in to 
protest the police decision.

My big questions to this lengthy prologue: 

Has anyone participated in a sit-in at a 
rave?  Do you think this is a productive tool to use when confronted by 
police?  Is a willingness to be arrested part of the raving spirit of 
protest?

I'm very curious to hear your responses and opinions.

Thanx, glad to be here, and Peace,

	-h-

From: csc@pilot.njin.net (Sean Casey)
Message-Id: <9403020013.AA20480@pilot.njin.net>
To: Henry Kumagai <uhdad@mcl.mcl.ucsb.edu>
Cc: sfraves@techno.Stanford.EDU
Subject: The sit-in to preserve raves
In-Reply-To: <Pine.3.89.9403011421.A22644-0100000@mcl.mcl.ucsb.edu>
References: <Pine.3.89.9403011421.A22644-0100000@mcl.mcl.ucsb.edu>
Sender: sfraves-owner@techno.Stanford.EDU

If it's a specific situation designed to test one's constitutional
right to peaceably assemble, I'm willing to join others in being
arrested and fight it in court. I believe very strongly that raves are
an important positive haven for those who might otherwise be on the
street causing trouble.

It's been brought up several times, but I'd really like to see more
efforts towards founding a church. As many people believe very
strongly in raves and the spirit of the thing (which can be written up
very simply and non-denominatory), there should be enough to get
something accomplished. When that is done, there are additional
constitutional issues involved in any police disruption, making it
(hopefully) less likely.

Is that not what a church is about? Promoting some ideal, assembly,
ritual, and celebration? I feel that if we want to do this, barring
excessive noise or gross issues of safety, we have every right to be
left entirely alone behind our closed doors.

I'll be happy to write some letters to various places in Santa Barbara
if someone can get me the addresses. For that matter, we need to start
letting the people in power know what a positive thing raves are. If
the UK can ban them, we'll be next.

Sean

Hmmm, I was also there, and I know the promoter involved, and he did not
call the cops, although I'm glad they shut it down. I believe (and I've
promoted events) that it was utterly irresponsible to throw an event of
that size without proper security. There was one security/doorman for about
300 people.(In a space that could comfortably hold about 200) When a female
friend of mine was assaulted and groped, the sole security guard, who is
also a friend of mine was understandably reluctant to do anything because
he had no backup. It was shocking and maddening to see the behavior there.
At this time the promoter wasn't even there. When he arrived and was told
what happened his attitude was "gee, too bad - not my problem."

Basically, while I'll give the Test promoter credit for doing a lot for the
SB scene, I don't think he "knows what it's all about." For him it seems to
be about money and attitude. An attitude of "I started the scene and there
isn't room for anyone else in this town." I've had a lot of great times in
SB, and it hurt to see what happened. But you can't place all the blame on
the cops, who were even cool enough to suggest ways of getting permits in
the future. I'd like to know where you heard that there were criminal
charges pending against those involved.

There were a number of reasons the cops gave for shutting it down, from
people hanging outside drinking beers, to lack of fire permits, to illegal
parking. And it sucks it got shut down, but if the event had been thrown
with a bit more consideration, it wouldn't have been. Look at Energizer,
thrown in the same warehouse last year (ironically, by the same people) -
the cops stopped by and didn't hassle anybody and the event went on all
night and it was a lot of fun.

> Here is my main question.  I was thinking afterwards that we should have 
> collectively sat-in to protect and assert our rights.  This was a peaceful 
> gathering at which one overzealous, high-strung person ruined it for everyone.  
> It frustrated me that I nor anyone else thought of the option to sit-in to 
> protest the police decision.
> 
> My big questions to this lengthy prologue: 
> 
> Has anyone participated in a sit-in at a 
> rave?  Do you think this is a productive tool to use when confronted by 
> police?  Is a willingness to be arrested part of the raving spirit of 
> protest?

I participated in a spontaneous sit-in, not at a rave, but here's the
situation.  In college, a group of ~80-90 people were peacefully
protesting on the lawn of the president, which was owned by the
College.  Many other protests had happened there without incident.
This time, however, the president had instructed them to remove
students by force if necessary.  We staged a peaceful sitdown strike,
at which point they grabbed a few random people by the hair and tried
to drag them off anyway.  (I was one of them, but was held back by the
crowd.)  Eventually, they succeeded in getting about six students into
the cars, and they would have had many more but for the rings of us
who formed around people getting kicked by the cops and kept them from
being arrested too.  Everyone spontaneously decided that they
weren't going to just drag a few scapegoats off to jail, so we blocked
the road on both sides and prevented the police from leaving.  After
roughly 20 more police cars showed up, including dogs and fire trucks,
and over a hundred more students showed to support those already in
the road, and several hours of negotiation ("Who's your leader?"  "We
don't have one."  "Let me talk to them."  "Who?"), the students were
all released, and we marched as a crowd to the police station and let
them know (non-violently) exactly what we thought of the whole thing.

The larger the group, the less likely you are to be arrested.  If there
is violence, you are about 100% likely to be arrested, so don't do that.

If you are touched by an officer, go limp and hope the crowd holds you
back.  Touching a cop WILL be construed as 'assault on an officer' in
court -- even tapping their arm.

Be willing to take consequences -- outdoors, trespassing, disturbing
the peace, and possible curfew; indoors, whatever weird local ordinance
applies.  IF YOU HIT BACK AT THE POLICE IN A PEACEFUL DEMO, YOU ARE 
FUCKED, AND SO IS YOUR CAUSE.

In short: If a sit-in is planned, make sure everyone knows what's going
on.  Make sure everyone knows what could happen to them and is prepared 
to deal.  Make sure everyone has the same plan of action because the
confusion will be intense.  Make sure you have a lawyer lined up
(check your ACLU for contact info).  Most importantly, make sure what
you're doing is right, that you believe in it, and that you're willing
to stay in for the long haul, because the legal system takes for-fucking-
ever.

I can try and explain more if desired.  Feel free to contradict me if you
have more experience...that was the only time I've dealt with cops actually
arresting friends, though I've been through other nonviolent demos which
didn't result in arrests (anti-war, ACT UP, etc.)

	The better your planning, the less likely there will be arrests
and bad press.  Remember that the whole thing is a publicity stunt -- it's
not for you, because you know you're right.  It's for the others
who think you're bad because the cops only harass bad people.  Choose
your strategy and act accordingly.

// g

PS:  Perhaps alpha-10, CC and/or other veterans have their two bits to
add...?

On Mar 1,  2:31pm, you proclaimed:
} Here is my main question.  I was thinking afterwards that we should have 
} collectively sat-in to protect and assert our rights.  This was a peaceful 
} gathering at which one overzealous, high-strung person ruined it for everyone.  
} It frustrated me that I nor anyone else thought of the option to sit-in to 
} protest the police decision.
} 
} My big questions to this lengthy prologue: 
} 
} Has anyone participated in a sit-in at a 
} rave?  Do you think this is a productive tool to use when confronted by 
} police?  Is a willingness to be arrested part of the raving spirit of 
} protest?

	A very interesting question!

	We in San Francisco have had it fairly easy when it comes to
parties, even though at times it doesn't feel like it.  Ravers in
England have had a very hard time with the police - Parliament in fact
has a bill before them which would all the cops to prevent the
assembly of 10 or more people at one time if they thought the purpose
was to have a "rave" (yes, that's the word used in the bill itself).
Ther have been some ugly busts there, tied in with the nomadic
Travelers/pagans.  (There are people on this list with direct
experience with this - speak up! :) Right now raves (a much aligned
word in the UK) exist either as clubs that go until 6am, massive
fully-permitted Bill Graham-style events tied in with other music
festivals, or highly illegal renegades (which is how this all started
anyways).

	Problems with the police have not been limited to that side of
the Pond - last year there was a big bust in Milwaukie (I think - the
reference is in the archives somewhere) where the police shut down a
party because fire safety laws were supposedly being broken, and then
proceeded to arrest and cite every single attendee with a $300 fine
for "aiding and abetting the illicit sale and consumption of alcohol",
because they found a beer bottle behind the DJ booth (talking about
grasping at straws!).  A massive, well-organized civil protest against
these fines (I think it mostly consisted of everyone walking in and
contesting the fine, and the courts refusing to hear each case one by
one) got them initially reduced and then charges dropped altogether.
I'll try and dig up that story too if there's interest.

	So, back to the situation at hand.  I don't think there's
much you can do about the criminal charges against your friend,
except look into all the laws involved, perhaps with the help of
a lawyer, and make sure there aren't any clauses or loopholes 
or unconstitutionalities which could help.  San Francisco's "no
dancing between 2am and 6am for those under the age of 21" law
was abolished thanks to protests from SF residents, partially
organized by promoters in the scene and a few SFRavers too.

	As for whether a sit-in would have been the answer, I would
say no, because there's not much a couple hundred people can do
against the well-armed militia our police force has become.  Back
in the 60's it might have been possible, but today's cops are in
NO mood to deal with stuff like this, and an act like that will
make them even less willing to hear you out.  Parties are a great
and wonderful thing, but one night's party isn't worth going to jail
over (choosing your battles wisely is important).  Also, people
didn't go to your party to become involved in a possibly life-
threatening situation.  There will almost definitely be some
who will be in no position to face figures of authority (too
much laughing as the officers sprout pig's snouts and pink ears :)
and that can only get you in more trouble.

	All the anarchists on the net will hate me for suggesting
this, but when the police the LAST thing you want to do is get
confrontational.  There is a definite science to cop assuaging.  Some
cops arrive with their course of action already determined, and
there's really not much you can do at that point except regroup and
either try and find a new location or call it a night.  *Most*,
however, (speaking from experience in the bay area, and this may not
be as true in other areas) can be cool, and with the right amount of
courtesy and respect and "Yes sirs", can be encouraged to let the
party go on.  This HAS worked - I've even heard some cops say "I
wish I could come join ya..." but it doesn't always.

	I wrote up a whole document about throwing parties outdoors
and dealing with cops effectively and such.  It's a MS Word for
Windows document (I thought I had a text version somewhere.. hmm.)
It's at ftp://techno.stanford.edu/pub/raves/spirit/iconekt.doc.
Lots of hints and stuff there.  

	Hope your friends come throgh this alright.  Send us updates!

	Brian

(Caution: I refer to my previous message a lot here.  If you got them
out of order you may be confused.)

> 	As for whether a sit-in would have been the answer, I would
> say no, because there's not much a couple hundred people can do
> against the well-armed militia our police force has become.  Back
> in the 60's it might have been possible, but today's cops are in
> NO mood to deal with stuff like this, 

Pshaw.  I've been at/in several demos in several cities, and if anything
the cops now seem to be less likely to beat the shit out of you because
they know cameras are watching and people don't trust them.  Back in the
60's you had people _killed_ by cops at Kent State and other places.

People still get killed by cops, but they don't get killed in big 
demonstrations -- they're killed in one-on-one (or several-on-one)
encounters where there are few or no witnesses.  Same with lesser
violence -- the probability of cop violence decreases as the
number of people increases.

> and an act like that will
> make them even less willing to hear you out. 

Like I said, sit-ins come after negotiation has failed.

> Parties are a great
> and wonderful thing, but one night's party isn't worth going to jail
> over (choosing your battles wisely is important).

This is the most important point, and I agree.  Civil disobedience is
publicity for your cause, not a cause in itself.

>  Also, people
> didn't go to your party to become involved in a possibly life-
> threatening situation.  There will almost definitely be some
> who will be in no position to face figures of authority (too
> much laughing as the officers sprout pig's snouts and pink ears :)
> and that can only get you in more trouble.

> 	All the anarchists on the net will hate me for suggesting
> this, but when the police the LAST thing you want to do is get
> confrontational.  There is a definite science to cop assuaging.  

Like you said, it's the _last_ thing you do, but sometimes it must be
done.  Like I said, plan carefully, and make sure no one gets trapped
into doing something they're not prepared to do.

// g

> Then people shit all over you, tell you to get a clue, ask you what

Nobody's slapping newbies, as I say now and have always said, I always tell
people who call me where the party is going to be, all they have to do is call
me.  Some of you can attest to this fact.  Lately it get's hectic around that
time.  I don't respond to e-mail because I am suppose to wait until about 10
p.m. before I tell anybody, between 10 and 11 is when I answer the phone,
I don't have time to answer individual e-mail (I would run an FMR list
if that were the case). BUT if you call between these times, there is a good 
chance that you will reach me and I will tell you where it is.  We want to
move the FMR further underground (that should be obvious), obviously the FMM
needs to solve some of it's own logistical problems (like more appropriate
startup times) but the whole idea is to have people come who _really_ want
to be there, it should be a personal thing, if you don't know anyone around
you that you can call, maybe you should reach out and touch someone :)
As I said before, this party is not just about Tanks of Nitrous and a great
way to start the weekend's festivities, we want people to come who can 
co-create the party.  That means meeting people and making an effort.
There are people on this list who live 3000 miles away and know how to find
the info, I know for a fact Sean that you know many people who you can call
to get the info. Once you have it you can take it upon yourself to disceminate
it.  We'd rather have it passed word of mouth then through the network,
(too easy to wind up on a BBS, which is why I don't run the fmr login
on nexsys anymore)  Since this has happened the parties have been smaller and
have been successful.  I think it should go even more underground.

There are no flyers for the FMR and there is no info line.  It really needs
to remain that way.  Two years ago, the people that came to the FMR were a lot
more responsible, they knew this was a potentially "illegal" action and acted
accordingly, they looked out for each other and came as a group.  Currently,
the parties have turned into Massives with 3 to 4 nitrous tanks, beer, fireworks
and other things that will bring the Authorities down on us in a hard way.
If the party was to continue, some changes needed to be made, so it's being
driven further underground, this last FMR had about 250 max, about 100 by 8 am.
The party before that had about 400 people.  Last Halloween we had about 
1600 - 2000 people at Bonny Doon.  It's better if the party is smaller.  It is
still open to anyone, you just have to make some effort to find it.  The 
effort we are asking is that you reach out with your heart and get to know
each other.  Times may soon come where these kind of relationships my prove
priceless and even lifesaving. 

Is that too much to ask?

Roxanna Stieber writes:
 > I for one will not post to someone that I haven't met face to face. How
 > do I know that they're cool? Just because you're on this list doesn't 
 > guarantee that.

This is worth discussing further. I take it Rox means "not a cop" by
being cool.

It's probably a good idea not to email the location around. Once the
information is online, it's just too easy to forward or post
somewhere. So I'd say it'd be a good idea to ask them to call you up.

But even if you insist on talking with someone, or meeting them in
person, it's going to be pretty hard to tell if they're connected with
law enforcement. And that's a tough dilemma.

At what point do we "protect what we have" by becoming exclusionary?
It's a really tough question. And just about any tactic one proposes
is going to come under criticism. I particularly like the approach
with the full moon events. Their tactic is to simply make it hard to
find out where it is. If you can find it, you're welcome to attend.

This *does* make it difficult for newbies to find it, which is okay by
me. The thing to do I think is offer to meet with the newbies and
carpool with them and introduce them around. Or if you personally
don't like newbies at FMRs, offer info that will help them get a
leg-up on the scene. Certainly no one is obligated to help newbies,
but I hope they can be given a positive first impression of things.

(That, by the way, is how you save the scene.)

When the problem is scaled down to smaller events, it gets a bit
stickier. If you don't get enough people, your party "fails". Get too
many, and your space may be trashed or you may attract police. How do
you get a decent crowd without having everyone in SF show up? Good
question.

I heard secondhand about someone (I don't know who) at Friends &
Family saying that they didn't like it because there were too many
people there they didn't know. I respect that person's opinion (just
as I respect DJs who tape over record labels), but I don't think it
helps things much. Many of us have found the scene a welcome refuge
from just this sort of idealogy. If the scene is important to you, you
might want to consider how such an idealogy would affect it in the
long term.

See you all tonight at C/U. If you don't know me, and want to meet me,
look for the blue (!) lightstick. I may have a red one too :). If you
don't know me and say hi, I may just give you one :).

Sean

PS - I'd be totally happy to spend time in the FMR phone tree, but I
live in Sacramento, and need to leave about 10pm, as it's a two hour
drive to most places in SF. I end up calling people from a pay
phone in SF to find out where it is. :(  I *really* need to move into
the city...

____________________________________________________________________

it is kinda good time, what with sfr being 2 yrs old and all. it is kinda
'taking stock' post.

 so it is 2 yrs since we've started this experiment in virtual community.
i felt for some time rather disconnected from the sfr and the
rest of the house community. i just want to let you know that i've felt
i've reconnected after the come/unity party at caesar's palace on the 19t
there are still some issues regarding how we relate to each
other and with the rest of the houserave community.
there are some problems which we have, methinks. by the very nature of
our medium, computers, we are alienated from each other. we need to take extra
efforts to stay 'connected.' also, i feel we are/have been rather disconnected
from  the larger house community. also, what sort of group are we ? a collectiv
 a top down structure ? several events recently highlight  this. the way
the sfr was brought to a halt was very much top down : brian just did it.
is this a communal type of decision making ? i think not. anyway, this
just points out to the fact that we've not really talked about how we
arrive at decisions.
the reasons ? well,  1st i point to the nature of the medium (see above).
also, i guess different people want different things out of sfr, so there r
varying levels of involvment. but , i also feel that this has to do
with a rather weak  kind of interaction   which we've had lately.
i should add that parties like ethan's and bryce's are good. but
i think we need to reach out the larger community as well.
some possible solutions: meet more regularly , say before going to events
together; also, discuss what kind of things do people want out of sfr;
post a monthly reminder of what sfr is/is becoming , as well as publicize
things like the fmr phone tree, sfr-orgamizers sublist, and how to get on it.
maybe we can wear sfr buttons more often.
i think this is a great endeavour we're on and want to keep it going,
to keep it growing.
take care/shalom
ovid
p.s. ok, i am ready, flame-proof :) shield on!

>        Lunches  are a good way for newbies to meet each other
>        and some of the old timers, that way  people will know what you look
>        like. And we'll know your not a cop (unless your an undercover and
>        in that case we'll  just have to convert you with love :) Another way 
>        to come to ComeUnity, or n/1994 or any of ethans or brycers parties.
>        These are (mostly ;-} legal house parties where it is unlikely that you
>        run the risk of getting arrested or hassled by the police.

This is exactly how I got connected with the SFR "movers and shakers."  My
friend Johnny had been on the mailing list for a number of months and we
went to events advertised on it, but it wasn't until he and I went to a
South Bay lunch that we got to know people who knew how to get connected to
the (former) FMR phone tree.  And to be honest, our initial reason to go to
the lunch was just to find our where the FMR was going to be.  But, you
know what?  He and I have made many close friendships that emanated from
that first lunch.

These friendships influenced me to move to San Francisco since I now had
*friends* in The City, not to mention instant access to all the events. And
though I've haven't been very active lately, I am really glad that I met
all of you as well as the people with whom I continue to make friendships.

Having said all those nice things, I'd like to mention something that seems
to be alluded to in the "Revealing" posts that intersects with this thread:
exclusivity.  Being somewhat new to the rave culture and somewhat
influenced by the 60's cultural movement, I was initially guarded but came
to fully appreciate the "welcome with open arms" approach that is being
espoused.  And, I came to understand that a certain level of secrecy needs
to be maintained.  I have been very lucky to be included in many secret
conversations because I happen to have befriended key people in SFR, but
I'm certainly not on the organizers mailing list (though I would love to
lurk on that one :)  ).  Yet, I believe there are many people on the list
that feel disco-nnected from the central aspects that make the SFR
experience so fantastic.

It's just too bad that secrecy and being "in" or "out" of various aspects
of the rave scene apparently contradicts the "everyone is welcome" ideal. 
All the reasons for secrecy have been well explained and justified.  I do
think it's funny that I've been dissed by ravers for having been part of an
exclusive, hierarchical fraternity when I see similar things going on in
rave organizations.  This is likely a human organizational phenomenon (get
out your organizational behavior textbooks :) ).  I am sorry but I'm just
one of those people who likes to go out and actively bring people into a
group that is doing great things.

I am simply proposing that we keep reminding ourselves to push as hard as
possible for *inclusion* wherever possible.  That is, not only creating an
environment that welcomes others, but we should actively and continuously
encourage others to be a part of us--in all aspects of the scene.  I see
this as work for a good cause.

Just thoughts from me and my Duo on a sunny rooftop in S.F...

>        Maybe it's time for Connection IV

Having missed, but heard about, I-III, I would definitely look forward to
it...and the C-IV tapes!!!


Rob Oliver

I think the problem cases showing up at the FMRs are almost positivly
NOT related to publicity/discussion/info distributed over the net.
Often they are locals (in the Bonny Doon case, a bunch of Santa Cruz
types) who keep the full moon date in mind and are notified through their
own networks, and keep the beer compressed and the nitrous chilled just
for the occasion.

As for the Authorities learning of the site through postings here or
elsewhere, feh.  In almost all cases the relevant LE types know about
this within minutes of the first cars arriving, sometimes sooner.
I mean, isn't this obvious?

I agree with Geoff wholeheartedly on one issue.  The FMRs need more
self-aware, massive-conscious participants.  Cutting the numbers may help
somewhat (larger numbers tend to generate different social dynamics anyway),
but *expecting* a higher level of consciousness is key to making it work.

Subject: Fwd: AP News Story about wired.com versus wire.net
Date: Wed, 02 Mar 94 17:42:37 -0800

I know this isn't a good place to discuss this, and I din't really want to
discuss it, nor do I think there should be any follow up discussion on this
here.

However, I thought I'd forward a 3rd party view of what happened, in order
to shed a little light on the subject of a recent sfraves flame war...

Personally, I think Wired Mag did the right thing in settling this stupid
bit of lawyerism, and Womens Wire certainly got a great deal of free
advertising through Wired Mag and through all the ensuing mediahype.

(Or maybe it was just a planned grab at the headlines for both parties!)

--------------------

>	This wire service news story illustrates the principle that
>one cannot simultaneously have very short names and grow the system to
>accommodate lots of organizations.  I expect this is just the first of
>what will become a torrent of legal conflicts over DNS names.  It's
>not that DNS names cannot be structured to provide a reasonable degree
>of descriptiveness through the use of a few layers of geopolitical
>qualification and use of longer strings.  The DNS name
>"cnri.reston.va.us" is a step in the right direction, but its unusual
>among domestic (U.S.) entries and even it does not spell out the full
>name of CNRI.  It is, however, the case that the Internet community
>has not forced such structuring of DNS names and thus we have problems
>of the sort described here. 
>
>Steve
>
>==================================================================
>Name Flap On Internet Raises Question Of Trademark Law
>
>By ELIZABETH WEISE= Associated Press Writer=
>	   SAN FRANCISCO (AP) _ Brand names and trademarks have long been
>so important to companies that they will protect them in court.
>	   Now, it appears, so has a computer address.
>	   In another sign of the growing importance and maturing usage of
>the Internet, two groups recently scuffled over the right to use a
>name that allows people to reach them on the big computer network.
>	   Wired magazine, a San Francisco-based monthly that covers
>digital technology, thought that the designation used by WIRE, a
>computer network devoted to women's issues also based in San
>Francisco, was too close to the one it uses.
>	   WIRE had wire.net. Wired has wired.com.
>	   The .net means that WIRE is a network. The .com designates a
>commercial venture.
>	   Lawyers were brought in, but the fight was solved last month
>without going to court.
>	   Wired agreed to pay half what it cost WIRE to change its name to
>Women's Wire and to run several ads for the network. In return,
>Women's Wire changed its name and its net address, which is now
>wwire.net.
>	   Rather than become a test case of trademark law in the
>electronic world, Women's Wire decided to settle so it could build
>its business, said development director Nancy Rhine.
>	   But devotees of the Internet say such a dispute shows its
>frontier days are ending.
>	   ``This was the first sign that there were a whole lot of legal
>matters that we haven't put our attention on, even though we knew
>that they're lurking there,'' said Howard Rheingold, author of
>``The Virtual Community,'' a recent book on the social effects of
>electronic networks.
>	   With new groups applying for names at the rate of a thousand a
>month, finding names that don't overlap is becoming more difficult.
>Already there are more than 60 domain names with ``inter-'' in
>them.
>	   There's no legal precedent that says whetehr domain names are
>like addresses, which can't be trademarked, or like names and phone
>numbers, which can.
>	   When a group wants to open a new Internet site, it applies to
>InterNIC for a domain name. InterNIC, the Inter-Network Information
>Center, has a contract from the National Science Foundation to
>assign and keep track of all domain names in the United States. It
>is operated by Network Solutions in Herndon, Virginia. Names are
>assigned on a first-come, first-served basis.
>	   Domain names actually correspond to numbers. The Internet
>address for Women's Wire is 192.190.111.25. Information stored in
>computer nodes throughout the net translate wwire.net to its
>address number so that people don't have to remember a 12-digit
>code.
>	   As more people use the network, some engineers believe a new
>address system will have to be devised.
>	   ``The technical gurus are busy trying to invent ways of getting
>around the current numbering scheme,'' InterNIC staffer Duane Stone
>said.
>	   As for trademark infringement, Stone says it's not InterNIC's
>problem.
>	   ``They expect me or somebody here to pronounce the final
>judgement and make it all right. But all we can do is say, `You
>guys need to come to some agreement of your own.' We're just a
>registry, not an enforcer.''
>	   InterNIC has no legal status at all, Stone said.
>	   ``Maybe someday they will be a court battle that will straighten
>it all out.''
>	   AP-DS-02-20-94 1555EST<
>
>

Warning:  soul-baring below.  If you don't care to read about
someone's personal angst in regards to the NET-rave scene, please
delete this.

Ovid writes:

>solutions: meet more regularly , say before going to events
>together; 

Heh.  I made a similar observation/recommendation a while ago right
before I moved here and you know what some people on SFRaves said to me:

	"I see computer people all day, I don't want to see
	more computer people when I go out."

	"Whenever I go out I see other SFRavers.  I'm getting tired
	of seeing the same people all the time."

	"Why should I come to an SFRaves party . . . I've got
	other friends throwing parties I want to support." (Geeze,
	it's just ONE NIGHT, you don't have to come to every single
	one of the darn things.)

I even posted descriptions of the kinds of things we did back East
in the net-rave community I was part of there, and people made fun
of me saying things like:

	"How can you dance if you spend so much time eating"
		(This after I described how we'd go out before and/or after 
		raves in New York, and how various ne-ravers used to bake
		cookies to share at raves.)

Over the past few weeks I've called my whole participation in the
net-rave scene into question.  Too many upsetting things have 
happened for me to even care what's going on most of the time.
I feel really hurt because in the beginning (two years ago) I
put so much of myself into net-raving, and watched a bunch of
weird people on the East Coast grow into a bizzare sort of extended
family, and then degenerate into a bunch of bickering kids trying
to show who is more "down with it" than the other.  

It hasn't been as bad here :-) but yet between various flame wars
and statements like the ones above I've been depressed to no end.

So anyway, I know I'm sitting here whining, but when I put up
suggestions for solutions I got stepped on so I'm feeling quite
bitter and empty of energy.  I feel like I've given all I can and
there's nothing left.  I did the best I knew how and got told I was 
wrong, that I can't possibly understand the scene here, and that I'm
on a power trip.  I realize the statements of a few individuals 
probably doesn't accurately reflect the tone of the 300+ sfravers,
but after all when the negative incoming mail is greater than the
supportive mail, then what am I supposed to think?

Laura

I found that there were many incredibly easy ways to meet tons of
ravers, both net and non-net:

Net: go to a party organized and promoted via the list, or one of the
lunches, or a concert (like the Orb) or to events which have been
heavily discussed on the list (like come/unity).

General: go to an event BY YOURSELF and be prepared to meet people.
It is amazing how many more people you meet when you go out by
yourself than if you go out with friends.

And yes, Martin, it IS that easy to meet people up here.  :-)
Bob

I'm not saying this is a bad idea or anythin, it's a good one, but I want
to point out some of the inconsistencies in the scene in general.  Every
time I've ever been at a party on full moon night, the minute someone finds
out the location it spreads like wild fire between groups and individuals
that are complete strangers.  Sometimes it is even announced over the mic.
I've also called and been given the location by people I know on this list
(bless them) who I know, but not too well.  I could be a cop or informant.
Oh ya, I even called the bartender at Zanzibar one particularly desperate
night and was given the location, no prob.  So.... I guess what I'm saying
is that security is only as good as the weakest link in the chain and there
art a lot of weak links out there.  People shouldn't get too uptight when
there are all these ways to find out that are easier or just as easy.  I
guess I'm also pointing out hints to the newbies so that they can find out
through these other methods.  This information can also be used by people
who want to attempt to clam down on these weak links.  Good luck.

-Mark

Susie says:

>Ne-ravers use badges...i have met a zillion ravers that way...need i
>say more

I'll agree.  There were so many times I'd be wandering around a rave and
I'd see that familliar bright pink badge.  I'd go up to the person, smile
and look at their email address on it, and I'd show them mine and the
end result was smiles and hugs from someone who I'd only known as an
email address.  That's how I met Susie's friend Doug, in fact:  he was
sleeping at a party and I went and read his badge . . . he woke up and
read mine, and it was like finding a long-lost cousin.  Best of all,
when the music is loud and it's hard to talk it's a good way to get
someone's name straight when you're meeting them for the first time.

Also, a lot of non-net ravers see the badges and ask what they are for.
So I tell them what's up and a number of them get very interested and turn
up on the email list shortly thereafter.  It's a lot more personal than
just handing out "come join our list" flyers, and it doesn't generate
any paper waste.

Of course, once when the subject was broached around here one of the
comments included "people don't necessarily want to be identified with
sfraves."  Whatever.  We certainly didn't MAKE anyone wear a badge,
we just made them available at cost to whoever on the list wanted one.

This all had a very good positive effect in the rave community:  
organizers and promoters would see how many net-ravers were really
around, and were impressed with the community we had created.  They'd
call one or another of us to make sure we all found out about events,
even very underground ones like Heather Heather's Back to Basics parties.
They really could see how we were supporting them, and were always
very positive and appreciative of our support.

Laura

From: "Susan R. Kameny" <susie@sutro.sfsu.edu>
> Ne-ravers use badges...i have met a zillion ravers that way...need i say 
> more...partyon...susie(:

Badges? Badges? We don' need no steenkin' Badges! :-)

w/r/t meeting people, I think it is far easier to meet SFravers than to
meet yer average club person. There's just something about dancing geek
to geek.

IMHO, about 50-75% of people at a rave fit  into the "club" or
"gangsta" persona and probably wouldn't have anything to do with me
since I don't spit-shine my shoes before going out and really couldn't
give a fuck about "looking like a raver" (generic baggy flannel person)
or doing raver drugs (X, Speed, Coke), or acting like i'm "down" with
any particular "scene" ...

But then again, i'm mostly uninterested in meeting people like that
anyways, so it all works out. I've never been able to maintain an 
idealistic streak that allowed me to believe that all people could
really/truly "come together into unity" (and since I don't do X, it's
pretty hard for me to hallucinate such a vision, though I have vague
recollections of how that felt in the past).

There's just too much engrained cliquishness, prejudice, and mistrust
among city-folks to make that happen in a real, deep, and true way. And
it seems to be an  unfortunate fact of human nature that we'd rather
label someone as "not one of us" than to try to understand and
appreciate them as an individual.

But, at least raves do bring lots of different people together, help
break down some of our social barriers, and allow us to meet people we
normally would never get to meet, etc. And that is a good thing, it is
real, and it's good enough for me.

For those of you on the calendar list, I want to let you know that
there is also a discussion list 'sfraves' for people send and receive
mail related to the 'scene' - the music, clubs, philosophy, etc.

To s*bscribe to the sfraves discussion mailing list,
send e-mail to majordomo@sfraves.stanford.edu with the line

s*bscribe sfraves

(type 'u' in place of the '*' - i've taken out the 'u' so this message
 passes thru majordomo's filter)

If you're on sfraves and only wish to receive the calendar, you can
also do that.  Follow the directions at the very bottom of the calendar.

arrivals   - funky buddha, check, gemini
departures - deep space?

Well I haven't seen any report yet so here we go:

Place: the Sounds Factory is a great place for a rave if the crowd is good 
(and last night it was very good). It was very nice to be able to dance 
with a fair amount of space around. It always nice to go back to a place
that hasn't been used in a while (at least for ravey kind of events)

Sound: what can I say --> It's the best sounds system that you can get in SF
by a long shoot.

Vibes: it's always hard to be objective but from mine impressions and from
my friends, it was one of those night that you can remember.

Music: I think last night Jeno, Simon and Garth really made a big effort to 
play something special (I get to hear these guys quite often but last night 
they definely put together a very interesting and "different from the usual" 
set). Jeno in particular was very strange, when I got there at 1:30ish he was 
playing some excellent techno. Simon was at his best in doing what he knows
how to do (you know those very acidy songs that build up in layers and blend
together in a way that you can't really tell which one is which).
Garth was unpredictable and very trippy. 

Good to see so many SFRAVES (Ovid, Lee, Robert, Comet, Sin, Derek, Sean, 
Crunch, Caliban and all the others that I forget now).


Thanks Malachi

					Giamma

i want to add  giamma's post. i agree that the sound is really great.
the musick was excellent and the vibe/crowd was good, pretty friendly.
tho' i stayed only until 4 am and was not using any psychoamplifiers,
the party felt really good, trancing out at times. actually i think
it turned out to be  good idea to change the place - remember, when
raves were at unusual places, unusal things can happen. i also loved
the visuals, synergy i belive. it really complemented the musick well.
how was dubtribe ? did they play ? they were supposed to in the
ambient room in the back, which was excellent. very conducive to spacing
out or to an intense discussion, like lee ani were having.
take care/shalom
ovid

	Or this information could also be used by any law enforcement
officials that might be on this list...  I don't mean to flame you by
any means, but don't you think that the cops probably know where the
FMRs are before half the people going there do?  If a FMR doesn't get
busted, it's not because the cops didn't find out about it, because
I'm sure there's not one of them they don't find out the location
for, but it's probably because they don't want to bother unless there
are too many people there or it's causing too much of a problem.  I
mean, the cop at Bonny Doon that gave everybody a parking ticket, I'm
sure he had to realize why all those cars were parked there
throughout the night.  Email and the net are probably the last 2
places the cops would go to find out.  They could get it much easier
other ways.
	But I do agree, if it were posted every time in sfraves, it would
make their job a lot easier.  Hell, they could even set up some type
of automated process that would immediately print out all messages
with the text strings "FMR" and "location" in them...  Then some
office law enforcement geek could sit by the printer and wait for the
paper to start feeding out...  Isn't technology great?  =)

Luke

No, you don't get off that easy, and since this is about the list I think it
is appropriate to discuss these issues here.  While it is true that at different
times, we have gone off the geneal subject completely, I think Brian has done
a reasonably good job at keeping the list on track, we don't need any list
gestapo to tell us what is appropriate behavior, if I want someone to tell me
what I can do, say, express, etc especially in the Scene then I can go out and
buy another TV and start watching again.  I think people self-police 
themselves pretty well.  And who says that this list EVER had a charter to 
host discussions about "the audio issues therein"? I know that is your agenda
and it is fine with me, I have benefited from your posts and think they are a
good thing, I just hope that you can see it clear to have a little bit more
tolerance to the type of things that others have to contribute to the community.

I thought C/U was pretty good. I was in kind of a moody mood, but I
still recognize it as a good event. There was just enough people there
for a killer vibe, but not so many it got truly uncomfortable.

When I got there Jeno was playing a fantastic tribal house set. I wish
he'd play more of the stuff because he is _really_ good at it. Later
he went into his usual Jeno house stuff. Simon really kicked my ass.
His set was pretty solid, and it was the exact kind of progressive
house I like to listen to (I only have 34 pieces of vinyl, he played 4
of them!). Hee hee. And Garth did his usually Garth house grooves,
getting just a bit weird and experimental near the end.

Bottles were confiscated. Free cups and free water inside. Free
oranges. No ins-n-outs. Nice promoters. $7, and well worth it.

Sean

ok, here's an alternative ComeUnity review - very different from those I've 
read.  I only got to the SoundFactory at 3:30am but when I got there the dance
floor was crowded.  The strange thing is that people seemed to be dancing for 
the sake of dancing, not because the beat was in them -- that was my 
observation.  A lot of slo-mo dancing was going on, nothing like the high 
energy I was used to at 1015 Folsom.

I personally think Sound Factory's acoustics suck.  So they spent a lot of 
money on their sound system... so what?  It was my personal opinion that the
treble was way too high and was masking over some of the bass.

The thing I really missed at this last Comeunity was a feeling of community.
Can't even be compared to the vibe that exists sometimes at a 1015 Folsom
Comeunity, in my humble opinion.

So I hung out at the chill room and blissfully listened to dubtribe.




Eyeballs, eyeballs, eyeballs,
    Spaced-out Funky EarthChild

Subject: sfr anniversary  , Meeting people
Sender: sfraves-owner@techno.Stanford.EDU

Ovid:
>there are some problems which we have, methinks. by the very nature of
>our medium, computers, we are alienated from each other. we need to take extra
>efforts to stay 'connected.'

There's a paradox here: our computers have helped us be much more connected
to people that we might have never interacted with at all.  On the other 
hand, this limited form of connectivity often fails to live up to the kinds 
of connections that many people would like to have.

> also, i feel we are/have been rather disconnected
>from  the larger house community. also, what sort of group are we ?

SFRaves is a communication forum, and nothing more.  But when people start 
communicating with each other, the idea of relationships tends to rear its
frightening head.  I guess what we can't help but to try to define the 
relationships that exist among us.  But this is no different than life-in-
general.  Trying to understand and define relationships is usually 
difficult, because people are complex and confusing.  In my life, I have
*never* found socializing  to be easy and without pain.  Nevertheless, 
raving and life with SFRaves has given me many opportunities to meet new 
people and it's been a lot fun.  It can be frustrating and alienating to 
have to deal with the limitations of these new relationships.  They can
often seem to be superficial and even insincere.  But the truth of the 
matter is that it takes a lot of time and energy for people to get deeply 
involved with each other.  It probably unfair for us to have high 
expectations from each other as far as social involvement goes.  But that 
doesn't mean it can't happen.

Laura:
>...when I put up
>suggestions for solutions I got stepped on so I'm feeling quite
>bitter and empty of energy.  I feel like I've given all I can and
>there's nothing left.

I'm still not sure I fully understand what the problems-in-need-of-solutions
are.  We all have different needs, and the chances are good that there will
be *some* SFR people who want to interact in similar ways and do similar 
things, unless what you want to do is incredibly weird or "non-ravey".  And 
even then, there are some incredibly weird and non-ravey people on this 
list.  So I wouldn't give up.  Also, there are new people coming in and out 
of our scene, so there's always the option of backing off, conserving 
energy, and trying again later. 

Disappointment seems to arise when people start expecting raving to fulfill
needs that go beyond the activity of raving.  I really can't blame people 
for wanting the rave scene to fulfill many of their social needs but the 
same thing happens with people who expect their career paths or family life 
to fulfill all their needs.  More often than not, they come up short.  

Crunch on meeting people in the rave scene:
>Well,  another method I fond VERY SUCESSFUL is to go to a LOT of raves.

That's right, Captain.  Sometimes I think we forget the obvious. Going to 
raves and putting out lot's of positive energy without any expectations is 
a good way to connect to other ravers.  When I was new to the scene, I 
would sometimes be dancing next to someone whose vibe & energy drew me 
toward them.  After a couple of hours, sticking my hand out and saying
"Hi, my name is Lee", was usually well recieved.  It isn't really that hard
to start talking to someone that you see (and has seen you) and shared
good feelings with.  

And likewise, if you want to meet more SFRavers, post more.  I guess
exposing oneself in public can be scary but once you've been reading the 
list for a while you have to realize that *everyone* who posts regularly
eventually says something stupid.   It's OK to be occasionally stupid.  
Stupidity is a big part of life.  In fact, I'd go as far to say the life
is generally stupid.  If you're posts are positive or informative or 
entertaining in some way, you're less likely to get flamed and like-minded 
people out there will *want* to meet you.  If you go to raves, review them 
and share an anecdote or two.  Every perspective adds something, even if it 
is stupid or boring.  

Another approach is to send people private email, especially if it is 
supportive or inquisitive about something they've posted.  We may not have 
much time or motivation to start a deep meaningful relationship, but even 
casual friendships can be fun.  When I'm out raving and I'm dancing near 
someone who's on the same wavelength as I, nothing needs to be said and 
we can feel pretty damn close.  Those feelings are very real, even if they 
are just of that moment. 

One final comment about SFRaves:  During the past 2 years there have been
a lot of successful cooperative efforts;  Connection I & II, Connection 
Earth, two Alternity's, a couple of major house parties, a few renegade
parties, dinners, lunches... and there are more things being planned.
Considering that we are a generally flaky and odd bunch, I think our 
erratic efforts at organizing and coming together have gone pretty well.  
Blaming the list for what it *hasn't* accomplished is sort of pointless, 
because SFRaves is not really a unified entity.  But we *always* have the 
option of reaching out, individual to individual.  SFRaves won't  make it 
happen, but it has given us the opportunity and some means to try.
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
Lee Fogel/\/\/\/\/\eVerything/\Is/\possiBlE/\/\/\/\/\lxfogel@srv.pacbell.com

I found the Sound Factory to be a bit too loud, and have too much
high-frequency sound energy at times. In any case, if you dance in the
middle you get very nice surround and good stereo definition. Anywhere
else in the room is mediocre at best. I forgot my earplugs so I did
the damp toilet tissue thing and it seems to work rather well, though
not as convenient to remove and replace as foam earplugs.

My subjective vote for best system goes to Sound Vision, which
*always* seems to be tweaked just right. I've been a high-end audio
enthusiast my whole life and I really appreciate clean sound with
punch. Volume is not everything!

Sean

> middle you get very nice surround and good stereo definition. Anywhere
> else in the room is mediocre at best. I forgot my earplugs so I did
> the damp toilet tissue thing and it seems to work rather well, though
> not as convenient to remove and replace as foam earplugs.
> 
> My subjective vote for best system goes to Sound Vision, which
> *always* seems to be tweaked just right. I've been a high-end audio
> enthusiast my whole life and I really appreciate clean sound with
> punch. Volume is not everything!

Well I don't want to start a "sound system" war but when I said that
that the Sound Factory sound system is the best, I was comparing
clubs sound system.
Sound Vision is great but you can't even compare it to the Sound Factory
or Townsend or any other big club. I mean, it's a pure matter of power.
You couldn't use Sound Vision at Townsend, it's way too small.
For smaller events I agree that Sound Vision is great, mainly because
Ethan does a good job in tuning it.

Saying that I'm not a "high-end audio" expert at all, what I really liked about
the Sound Factory is that even if the volume is very high (and it was definely
high at C/U), you can stay in front of the speakers and survive. At 
Townsend if you stay in front of the speakers you gonna have pain in your ears.

Anyway I'll be at Wicked on Saturday so I'll give to Townsend another chance :)

BTW is Wicked in the same place where Rhythem is, or is it gonna be at King St ?


					Giamma

No flame taken.  And I sort of agree, that's was sorta the point of my post.
I personaly believe that there are many ways for the cops to find out if they
realy want to.  That said, I also could conceive of them not realy caring to
go out of their way to find out but e-mail making it so easy that it tips
the decision making scales in the favor of finding out.  I also have mixed
feelings about wheather there is an organized law inforcement interest.  If
they realy were after us they could easily shut it down, or at least aggresivly
try, which they don't.  The fmr's are held in at least 3 or 4 different
counties in many different city jurisdictions, that means some higher athority
would have to be involved to surveil and then report to which ever city/county
it were going to be held, at which time the police would be there before or
during our arrival because they would find out at the same time and have less
distance to travel to get there.  Or, they would at least come by and check
things out if they were going to "let" us have it.  That's what they do when
they find it and "let" us keep going.  That's another point, sometimes they
never come, sometimes they come very late/early like at 3 or 4 and if you've
ever been one of the people there when they first pull up they seem real
clueless as to what's going on.  It's obvious that they just stumbled on to
it and don't know what the hell is going on, unless they're better actors
then I give them credit for.  Then there are the couple times that they were
there early enough that it seems kind of suspicious.  Who's to say?

-Mark

 When Elizabet ( pre-baby ) started the South Bay lunch two years (?)
ago , it was just a way for SFRaves folks in the South Bay area to
meet and socialize for awhile. Any folks from the distant Northern
climes that managed to make it to the lunch were warmly greeted as
a pleasant surprise.

 There was a nice weekend lunch we did in Mountain View , in the park
next to Geoff White's apartment complex. Bob Ramstead's rave-in-a-bag
sound system was checked and approved by a passing park cop , so
we where able to have music during our conversation. Also ,it being
on the weekend , most of us didn't have to worry about work or classes.

 Maybe we can repeat something like that again , on a weekend , higher
north on the peninsula. We've had North Bay and South Bay lunches - how
about someone suggesting/finding a place for a weekday or weekend
Peninsula lunch?

Message-Id: <Pine.3.85.9403051522.A20531-0100000@netcom3>
Mime-Version: 1.0
Content-Type: TEXT/PLAIN; charset=US-ASCII
Sender: sfraves-owner@techno.Stanford.EDU


Well whatever they're called au'-tek-er...oh-tik'-re...(I personally just 
call 'em okra :) ), they were damn good. I wasn't sure how long the set 
was, but I think it was around 45 mins or so. I didn't recognize anything 
from Incunabula and it seemed like they were doing quite a bit of 
music-arranging on the fly. It was definitely a dancing set. There were 
only two or three actual pauses in the music. Most things just flowed 
from one piece right into the next. Nicely done. Unfortunately there were 
only a handful of people really dancing to them or even listening to 
them. Funny, but I noticed that several of these folks were either 
sfraves people or SpaceLabbers. Go figure. Regardless, my friend in 
Chicago (their Sunday show) got a late night call commanding him to 
attend.

Well, that was the bright side. Granted it may be a personal grudge, but 
I don't care for Sound Factory very much. The new interior was nice, but 
crowd doesn't seem to have improved. Clubs in D'troit are pretty sketchy, 
but I'd never been hassled too much there. Last December while going to 
visit my roomate on her last night bartending there, I got cold-cocked 
for eight nifty stitches right on the corner. Inside, I hate to say it, 
but it's really a meat-market. Carmen got physically bugged several times 
while we were there, and ended up really not enjoying the evening. So 
after Autechre (Okra) got off the stage we split. I suppose it's just a 
problem with the 'big weekend club' in general, but Sound Factory has 
been hurting for money since it opened and has basically taken any party 
to come its way (Saturday nights were really scary there last year) 
regardless of safety or attitude. Dave Dean's a nice guy and probably 
means well, but at the scale of SF, I think money tends to eclipse all 
other concerns. Sad, but we won't be back there any time soon.

JMAR.

Tribal Funk
===========

Hah!!  All you SFRaver weenies missed a real good one.    I know it
was located at College Of San Mateo (A killer space),  and very hard
to get to if you don't have a car,   but just the same,   it wasn't that
well attended my any SFRavers,   at least by those that I know.

They had big floor fans,   which made the dance vs Chill ratio way up
there,  which really allowed me to dance my rear end off.  Yowsa!!
What total positive energy it had.    Great crowd,  and the CSM police
were very present but not at all harrassing people at least from any
position I might have seen.    Security was by the Lions Security,  which
is in my books,  very good.    There was plenty of free water,   and
two dance areas fed by the same music source.

The Darwin Chamber provided killer ambient sounds,  and the lazer was
super great and killer,  and aimed AWAY from innocent eyes.

Unfortunately,  it wasn't that well attended,  which is VERY unfortunate
for Flash the Promoter.    Garth finished up the set,  with a killer funkey
set of tunes,   quite different than he did at Come/Unity last Wednesday.

Anyway,   Tribal Funk was very good,  in a great killer space,   good
security,  and towards the end everyone gathered in a great big circle
running in and out,   clapping hands,  in one big cynergetic dance
that was pure unity,  good vibes,  and great fun for all.

Cap'n Crunch

There's a theory that goes something along the lines of, information
has no value in of itself, its value only exists in the delta, in the
passing of this information from one incarnation to another.  A
corollary is that information easily transmitted is often undervalued.
If you knew the ending to a murder mystery before reading the book,
you probably wouldn't waste your time reading the rest of it.  It's
that suspense, that tension, that gives the ending its punch.  And
in much the same way, the thrill of attending an outdoor party can
be directly proportional to the effort expended to find it.

The Wicked full moon parties can be very rewarding experiences.  But
their magic is hardly unique - there are other outdoor parties, and
they often hit the same level.  And if you can't find any that suit
you, and you have the energy - throw your own! Lots of people on this
list have - and I've collected a few of their posts on the subject
on the archive site in its own directory.

	Brian

they definitely played an extended version "Crystel" (off of Artificial

going on and very few stayed in to watch.  Although, a few clueless types

people were facing the stage. (In all fairness, the stage was sometimes
dark during the show and a drunk person may not have understood something
was going on based upon this lack of a clue, I mean, cue. :) )  SFRavers I
knew in attendance were Johnny T., Sin, Brian Sassone, and Ovid. Sorry that

>Well, that was the bright side. Granted it may be a personal grudge, but

Yeah.  After Autechre, the room quickly filled with clubbers and the vibe
basically went down the drain. Fortunately, some vibe existed due to the
minority presence of raver-types. :)  And, I'm sorry, but who ever spun
(Buck?) was playing way too housey and commercial stuff; it was painful for
me to remain there another 2 hours! (But, I do think the sound system at
Sound Factory was very, very clean...and a "good" kind of loud.)

And, did you know that some special guests were in attendance?  Yes! 
Daniel Ash and David J. came to see Autechre, but were a little too late to
see 'em.  I only know this because this cool, Iranian guy (Mehdi) from New
Yawk, who we met in line, recognized Daniel and went up and chatted with
him.  To his surprise, Daniel offered him a hit of his spleef (which the
nearby security guard ignored :) ).  Johnny went up and asked Daniel (in
his typical fashion) "why are you here?," and he said that they were in
S.F. to record a Love and Rockets album.  It looked to me like Daniel and
David were having a *great* time!  It will be interesting to see if the
S.F. scene will influence their music.

Your roving reporter,

Rob

I just moved to the Bay Area a few weeks ago from Boston via L.A. & 
Tokyo. This is my first posting to the list but I have been reading it 
for awhile and have been involved with alternative culture 
internationally for the past 4-5 years. I'm an ambient/experimental DJ 
and former General Manager & Program Director of WZBC-FM, Boston.

I saw Autechre on Fri. They were very good but the crowd was lame at the 
Sound Factory. Oh well what do you expect. Lets jus hope that we start 
seeing acts of this quality at our "underground" events.

 Re the Sound Factory Sound System- Kind of like Las Vegas or the Digital 
Superhighway- Flashy, lots of hype but little _elegance_

Re: Love and Rockets-

I entered the venue at the same time as the Rockets and told them that 
there was a good band playing. They had wangled there way in for free by 
name dropping to the guy at the door. Thats cool... They didn't seem to 
hip to techno/house/rave culture but they knew what was up and seemed to 
enjoy the quality performance. 

I was originally going to go to Tribal Funk's Feel The Groove but I 
didn't have a ride. I had hoped that the Sound Factory would have been 
tolerable after the Autechre performance but the hardcore booze/pickup 
vibe drove me from the venue immediately following the performance. Oh well.

Hope to start being more active on the scene soon. Lots of LOVE.....
Look forward to conspiring with kindred spirits.

					Ronan (aka RaZome)

Subject: Wicked!!!!
Sender: sfraves-owner@techno.Stanford.EDU

Wicked was all THAT!!!

I didn't get there in time to hear Garth's set (no biggie), but I sure as
fuck heard Jeno, Thomas, and Markie (The MAN) Mark.

Jeno's set - Brilliant!  He was in a good mood, so he was less experimental
than usual, but a killer set nonetheless.  Jeno, of late, is getting his
form, and stride back!  He worked the decks and showed why he is still a
force to be reconed with.  However, I was dismayed at the fact that he did
not use the third turntable.  3 TT mixing is the shit.

Thomas' set - Good one!  I hardly hear Thomas anymore except for Wicked, so
it was a pleasure to hear him mix it up!

Markie's set - Markie, bugger off!!!  Before Markie went on I *told* him,
"Take it easy, ease into your set, give my brain a chance to adjust"  and
*yes*, he listened...payed no attention...then he FUCKED IT UP!!!  Goddam
Markie!!!  He played shit from dimension triple D.  And was relentless to
boot!  He got those highs cranked, then bottomed them out with
bass...motherfuckers were tranced out (can I get a witness Geoff), and
really stupid.  It was great.  As I previously predicted trip-hop is in the
*house*.  Just ask Markie!

Wicked is proving to be alot more than anything I previously gave credit
to.  There are hella friendly people there, lots of *flavor*, and
*excellent* mix of people (old/new skool, newcomers, gays/straights/bi's,
every color under the rainbow...in other words a *REAL* party!).

Previous accolades aside, the crowd did have soem strange energy.  There
are a couple of people who bug the hell outta me even when I catch a
glimpse of.  I can *only* imagine what they think of me.  In any case, I
had one dude bug out in front of me, and it was hard to ignore (I despise
tweekers).  The others are denizens of a different sort.  Despite this
minor pet peeve, everyone seemd to have a good time.

Shit to watch:  Stippers. I do love to watch them at Boogie and even at a
party or two.  But when they come as a posse, and get that good belly
going, and booty shaking at 200 bpm (bumps&grinds per minute), it's time to
call in the cavalry!

It's really good to see sooooo many sistahs out these days. Renews my faith
in what this shit is all about.

It is also cool to see soo many happy people out.  Finally the smiles are
returning.  After last weeks fiasco at FMM, this event erased any doubt
that the *boys* are back.  Wicked!!!

FMM/WIcked:
===========
These are two seperate events that just happen to include, *at times*,
similar personnel.  The vibe is completely different, and so is the crowd.
I dunno wassup with people bundling the two just because some people are in
both camps.  I think it is unfair to the repective parties (i.e Wicked or
FMM) to bundle them.  I think FMM is a good vehicle for Wicked promo, but I
would just as well leave it at that and not make any additional corrolaries
since they do try to make each event stand on it's own merits.

Pax,
John


P.S.  Markie should be placed under HOUSE arrest!!!!

	actually it was discovered that japanese yakuza were using both
	Thunder Bay and 1015 as a front for selling the very illegal
	and expensive Blue LEDs. They had been trafficking them for years.
	The FBI as about to bust both locations however during their 
	investigations they uncovered several CIA agents which were involved.
	They were unable to determine whether the CIA was actually 
	investigating or if they were the criminals themselves so the FBI
	collaborated with Interpol and MOSSAD and together investigated and
	found that the CIA actually were *buying* the Blue LEDs to use in
	their own espionage efforts in Japan to uncover a Yakuza plot
	to corner the market on illegal Blue LEDs.

	in the end the Blue LED market crumbled and the syndicates lost 
	interest in running their front clubs- 1015 and Thunder Bay so they
	took off to another city to set up business.

	be careful of those expensive and illegal Blue LEDs. they have
	already caused the shutdown of two expensive and poorly ventillated
	nightclubs.

After checking around it seems that 1015 was shut down due to, now dig
this, not having permits for after-hours (could be why they've been sweated
for awhile)!  And they have been operating for SOME TIME without permits.
The catalyst for all the hoopla? having a party where people were on the
roof.

So, as you can guess, after hours will be held in other spots.

For more flakiness...some people (read promoters) have decided NOT to do
afterhours whatever, and others have decided to jump in.  Since the shit is
in such a deep state of flux, there is little reason to name names (esp.
since everyone keeps changing their minds on a daily basis).

Anyone check out the after hours at 1190 Folsom?  I was NOT gonna go,
primarily because the people doing the promoting (flyering [???]) didn't
know SHIT about the party.  Hell, if you're handing out flyers, the least
you can do is know WHAT you're promoting for.  I guess some people need a
crutch to get into parties for free; I think it's a cheeseball tactic.  So
if anyone went, lemme know.

Hows that DNA horse doing. Any good?

Pax,

Subject: week in revu

Wed - ComeUnity - I arrived at the Sound Factory with much trepidation;
I've never had a good time there before so i was prepared to just
checkitoutandleave.  I got there early, around 1, and things seemed to be 
going off pretty well: Jeno was doin' fine, the sound system was good, and 
most of the CU regulars were there.  To me, the layout of the Sound F. is
kind of cold and divisive with too many separate rooms and a long hallway 
to a barren chill space.  But by 3 the place had filled up nicely with 
friendly, happy people.  Although not as magical as other CU's I've been 
to, I ended up having a pretty good time, staying until 6-ish

Sat - Wicked - Still feeling a bit drained from CU, I dealt with my
weekend-club-crowd phobia by not arriving until 4.  This party was 
definately going off full blast when I got there, and the vibe steadily 
improved as the less devoted and less enhanced drifted off.  One thing I 
really liked about Townsend was the lighting - stimulating and not 
overdone, and the entire space was light enough so that for once I was 
able to actually *see* everyone's faces within 20 feet of me.  I felt that
this really helped keep the spirit alive.   Martin - if you're reading 
this - please consider ways to lighten up your parties a bit.  At the last 
Gathering we did an experiment and discovered that you could not discern a 
smile on a face that was more than 6 feel away.  People can't feed off of 
each other's energy if they can't see each other.  Anyway, Wicked 
definately rocked and Townsend's great sound more than compensated for 
their anal security personnel.

Sun. - Bulletproof Boat Party - Fun as usual, although for me, the music 
was basically horrible.  I'm just not much of a fan of retro 70's disco, 
hip hop, or acid jazz played through a lousy sound system.  Still, it's 
hard not to have a good time partying on a boat with friends with the Bay
Area's glittering panorama as a backdrop.  But until the music starts 
coming closer to my tastes, this party will have to remain a 
once-every-few-months novelty.
  
It sounds like the FunkyTechnoTribe people are going to throw another
freebee at the Bandshell this weekend; they've been getting postponed
due to weather but seem determined to do another one on Saturday "rain
or shine".  Call their info line - 4152668215 for updates.  

ComeUnity and Wicked were really good parties but I can sense that I'm 
starting to force it a bit.  I think I need to be outdoors more.  Nothing 
beats raving with nature, so with the weather getting warmer I'm probably 
going to start holding out for outdoor events.   We'll see.
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
Lee Fogel/\/\/\/\/\eVerything/\Is/\possiBlE/\/\/\/\/\lxfogel@srv.pacbell.com

hi lee, sfr folx,
well, thought i'd tell my story of the week too. i really like c/u,
definitely like the musick more. it is this mix of house-y stuff on top
with some HARD beats underneath. what do people call this ? progressive ?
whatever it is called, i likes. the c/u dj's were playing it and so were
the wicked folx. there is also this song which is hip-hoppy and somewhere
in the middle it just goes 1/2-time. does anyone know who is that ?
so c/u was good, tho' could not stay that late - had to come in
before noon into work. did go to s faktory on fri. i really dug autechre,
with their hard beats and ethereal lines. i like that tension  which is set
up this way. as for the dance afterwards, yes, i agree i did feek like usda
prime cut ( well, not really, i think women get more crusied that way than
men do ) so that kinda spoiled the atmosphere a lot. tried to meet some
people with mixed success. as for the musick , it was very inconsistent,
at times really good, then next really bad. could not really get a groove
going. so was tired and unsatisfied , like after 5 hrs. of foreplay!
anyway, wicked on sat. definitely went off, as most people have already said.
the afterhrs did not happen at 1190, so went to dna instead. that was
GREAT. i loved the visuals - btw, how come there are fewer visuals at parties?
they had cool visuals, including some stuff with terrance mckenna. and the
musick! the musick was really good, again this hard trance over ambient or
house. so what's up, did several dj's get new crates of musick? it sounds good
tho' am not complaining. there were not that many people, but it seemed to
pick up again around noon when i left on sun.
take care/shalom/(se)X hits the spot

vinyl I own :).

Subject: Leahy to hold hearings on Clipper Chip!
 
 
Dear Friends on the Electronic Frontier:
 
I have some good news to share with you.  Senator Leahy just sent me a
letter indicating that he *will* be scheduling hearings on the
Administration's Clipper Chip proposal.  I would like to thank all of you
who sent us messages to forward to him urging hearings.  I'm sure that
stack of messages we printed out made a significant impact on the Senator
-- the stack was over seven inches tall!  (We look forward to the day when
no trees will have to be sacrificed in the furtherance of democracy!)
 
And if you haven't written a message to Rep. Cantwell yet about her
proposed amendment to the Export Control Act, please do so and forward it
to cantwell@eff.org.  This is an address we set up to enable us to collect
messages in support of her bill.  We have been printing out messages and
delivering them each week -- so far we've received over 4500 letters of
support.  For more information on the Cantwell bill, send a message to
cantwell-info@eff.org.
 
Thanks again.  We'll let you know as soon as the Clipper hearing gets scheduled.
 
Sincerely,
 
Jerry Berman
EFF Executive Director
 
-.-.-.-.-.-.-. forward from Sen. Leahy -.-.-.-.-.-.-.-.-.-.
 
 
United States Senate
Committee on the Judiciary
Washington, DC  20510
 
March 1, 1994
 
Mr. Jerry Berman
Executive Director
Electronic Frontier Foundation
1001 G Street, Suite 950 East
Washington, DC  20001
 
Dear Jerry,
 
Thank you for forwarding to me the many thoughtful and informative messages
you received over the Internet regarding the Administration's recent
approval of an escrowed encryption standard, known as the Clipper Chip.
 
Many of the messages urge Congress to hold hearings to review the
Administration's Clipper Chip standard.  In fact, I intend to hold a
hearing before the Judiciary Subcommittee on Technology and the Law, which
I chair, to consider the important issues raised by the Clipper Chip.  I
will let you know when a date for the hearing is scheduled.
 
Thank you again.
 
Sincerely,
 
/s/ PATRICK J. LEAHY
United States Senator
 
PJL/jud

JOIN EFF!!
==========
 
EFF's work as a civil liberties organization in Washington has been very
successful, but the realization of our goals of freedom and privacy online
can only come with the active and vocal participation of the entire online
community.  Now that you have personally experienced both the threat of the
loss of your privacy and the power having won the first battle, won't you
take that next step and become a member of EFF?
 
By joining EFF, you will help us to expand our reach to educate and involve
an even greater number of people in the shaping of these critical issues.
Your tax-deductible donation will tie you into the EFF information network
and support our public policy and legal work.  As a member, you will be
guaranteed timely the timely information and mechanism you need to respond
on these issues.  Our voices in unity *do* make a difference.
